Relevant discussion may be found on the [talk page](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Talk:Suez_Crisis "Talk:Suez Crisis"). Please help [improve this article](https://en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=Suez_Crisis&action=edit) by [introducing citations to additional sources](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Help:Referencing_for_beginners "Help:Referencing for beginners").

_Find sources:_["Suez Crisis"](https://www.google.com/search?as_eq=wikipedia&q=%22Suez+Crisis%22)–[news](https://www.google.com/search?tbm=nws&q=%22Suez+Crisis%22+-wikipedia&tbs=ar:1)**·**[newspapers](https://www.google.com/search?&q=%22Suez+Crisis%22&tbs=bkt:s&tbm=bks)**·**[books](https://www.google.com/search?tbs=bks:1&q=%22Suez+Crisis%22+-wikipedia)**·**[scholar](https://scholar.google.com/scholar?q=%22Suez+Crisis%22)**·**[JSTOR](https://www.jstor.org/action/doBasicSearch?Query=%22Suez+Crisis%22&acc=on&wc=on)_(August 2023)_

The great military lesson that was reinforced by the Suez War was the extent that the desert favoured highly fluid, mobile operations and the power of aerial interdiction. French aircraft destroyed Egyptian forces threatening paratroopers at Raswa and Israeli air power saved the IDF several days' worth of time. To operate in the open desert without air supremacy proved to be suicidal for the Egyptian forces in the Sinai. The Royal Marine helicopter assault at Port Said "showed promise as a technique for transporting troops into small landing zones".[[155]](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Suez_Crisis#cite_note-Varble,_Derek,_p._91-160) Strategic bombing proved ineffective.[[257]](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Suez_Crisis#cite_note-Varble,_Derek,_p._92-264)

Revise Phase II failed to achieve its aim of breaking Egyptian morale while at the same time, those civilian deaths that did occur helped to turn world opinion against the invasion and especially hurt support for the war in Britain. Egyptian urban warfare tactics at Port Said proved to be effective at slowing down the Allied advance. Finally, the war showed the importance of diplomacy. Anglo-French operations against Egypt were militarily successful, but proved to be counterproductive as opinion in both the home front in Britain and France and the world abroad, especially in the United States, was against the operation.[[257]](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Suez_Crisis#cite_note-Varble,_Derek,_p._92-264)

### Europe

In West Germany, the Chancellor [Konrad Adenauer](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Konrad_Adenauer "Konrad Adenauer") was shocked by the Soviet threat of nuclear strikes against Britain and France, and even more by the quiescent American response to the Soviet threat of nuclear annihilation against two of NATO's key members. The Bulganin letters showcased Europe's dependence upon the United States for security against Soviet nuclear threats while at the same time seeming to show that the American nuclear umbrella was not as reliable as had been advertised.[[258]](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Suez_Crisis#cite_note-FOOTNOTEDietl2008273%E2%80%93274-265)[_[neutrality](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Wikipedia:Neutral\_point\_of\_view "Wikipedia:Neutral point of view") is [disputed](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Talk:Suez\_Crisis "Talk:Suez Crisis")_]

As a result, the French became determined to acquire their own nuclear weapons rather than rely upon the Americans, while Germany became even more interested in the idea of a European "Third Force" in the Cold War. This helped to lead to the formation of the [European Economic Community](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/European_Economic_Community "European Economic Community") under the 1957 [Treaty of Rome](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Treaty_of_Rome "Treaty of Rome"), which was intended to be the foundation of the European "Third Force".[[258]](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Suez_Crisis#cite_note-FOOTNOTEDietl2008273%E2%80%93274-265) The European Economic Community was the precursor to the [European Union](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/European_Union "European Union").

### Egypt

[](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/File:Nasser_and_Quwatli_clasp_hands.jpg)

Presidents [Shukri al-Quwatli](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Shukri_al-Quwatli "Shukri al-Quwatli") (left) and Gamal Abdel Nasser (right) clasp hands in front of jubilant crowds in [Damascus](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Damascus "Damascus") days after the union of Syria and Egypt into the [United Arab Republic](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/United_Arab_Republic "United Arab Republic"), 1958

With the prompt withdrawal of UK and French troops, later followed by Israeli troop withdraw, Egypt kept control of the Suez Canal.[[18]](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Suez_Crisis#cite_note-Economist2006-23) After the fighting ended, the Egyptian Chief-of-Staff [Abdel Hakim Amer](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Abdel_Hakim_Amer "Abdel Hakim Amer") accused Nasser of provoking an unnecessary war and then blaming the military for the result.[[259]](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Suez_Crisis#cite_note-Kandil2012p50-266) The British historian [D. R. Thorpe](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/D._R._Thorpe "D. R. Thorpe") wrote that the outcome gave Nasser "an inflated view of his own power",[[116]](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Suez_Crisis#cite_note-Thorpe-121) thinking he had overcome the combined forces of the United Kingdom, France and Israel, failing to attribute their withdrawal to pressure from the superpowers.[[116]](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Suez_Crisis#cite_note-Thorpe-121)[[260]](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Suez_Crisis#cite_note-Varble,_Derek,_p._84-267)

Nasser emerged a hero in the Arab world. American historian Derek Varble commented, "Although Egyptian forces fought with mediocre skill during the conflict, many Arabs saw Nasser as the conqueror of European colonialism and Zionism, simply because Britain, France and Israel left the Sinai and the northern Canal Zone."[[260]](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Suez_Crisis#cite_note-Varble,_Derek,_p._84-267) The historian Andrew McGregor argued that the retreat from Sinai was not a complete rout, since it preserved most of the regular army for fighting the larger enemy—Britain and France.[[261]](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Suez_Crisis#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMcGregor2006256-268) Historian [P. J. Vatikiotis](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/P._J._Vatikiotis "P. J. Vatikiotis") described Nasser's speeches in 1956 and after as providing "superficial explanations of Egypt's military collapse in Sinai, based on some extraordinary strategy" and that "simplistic children's tales about the Egyptian air force's prowess in 1956 were linked in the myth of orderly withdrawal from Sinai. All this was necessary to construct yet another myth, that of [Port Said](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Port_Said "Port Said"). Inflating and magnifying odd and sporadic resistance into a [Stalingrad-like](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Battle_of_Stalingrad "Battle of Stalingrad") tenacious defense, Port Said became the spirit of Egyptian independence and dignity."[[262]](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Suez_Crisis#cite_note-269)

During the Nasser era, the fighting at Port Said became a symbol of Egyptian victory, linked to a global anti-colonial struggle.[[263]](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Suez_Crisis#cite_note-270) Of Nasser's post-Suez hubris, Thorpe wrote, "The [Six-Day War](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Six-Day_War "Six-Day War") against Israel in 1967 was when reality kicked in—a war that would never have taken place if the Suez crisis had had a different resolution."[[116]](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Suez_Crisis#cite_note-Thorpe-121) Of [Tawfiq al-Hakim](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Tawfiq_al-Hakim "Tawfiq al-Hakim")'s writings about the 1956 and 1967 wars, Vatikiotis summarizes: "Were bluffing and histrionics in the nature of Nasser? It was bluffing that led to the crushing of Egypt in 1967, because of the mass self-deception exercised by leaders and followers alike ever since the non-existent 'Stalingrad which was Port Said' in 1956."[[264]](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Suez_Crisis#cite_note-271)

#### Crackdown on Egyptian Jews

Further information: [1956–1957 exodus and expulsions from Egypt](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/1956%E2%80%931957_exodus_and_expulsions_from_Egypt "1956–1957 exodus and expulsions from Egypt") and [Jewish exodus from the Muslim world §Egypt](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Jewish_exodus_from_the_Muslim_world#Egypt "Jewish exodus from the Muslim world")

In October 1956, when the Suez Crisis erupted, Nasser brought in a set of sweeping regulations abolishing civil liberties and allowing the state to stage mass arrests without charge and strip away [Egyptian citizenship](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Egyptian_nationality_law "Egyptian nationality law") from any group it desired; these measures were mostly directed against the [Jews of Egypt](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/History_of_the_Jews_in_Egypt "History of the Jews in Egypt"). As part of its new policy, 1,000 Jews were arrested and 500 Jewish businesses were seized by the government.[[265]](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Suez_Crisis#cite_note-272)

A statement branding the Jews as "Zionists and enemies of the state" was read out in the mosques of Cairo and Alexandria. Jewish bank accounts were confiscated and many Jews lost their jobs. Lawyers, engineers, doctors and teachers were not allowed to work in their professions. Thousands of Jews were ordered to leave the country.[[266]](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Suez_Crisis#cite_note-Laskier_1995,_p._581-273)

They were allowed to take only one suitcase and a small sum of cash, and forced to sign declarations "donating" their property to the Egyptian government. Some 25,000 Jews, almost half of the Jewish community, left Egypt, mainly for Israel, Europe, the United States and South America. By 1957, the Jewish population of Egypt had fallen to 15,000.[[267]](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Suez_Crisis#cite_note-JVL-274)

### Britain

[](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/File:Nasser_and_Macmillan.jpg)

Nasser and [Harold Macmillan](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Harold_Macmillan "Harold Macmillan"), 1960

The political and psychological impact of the crisis had a fundamental impact on [British politics](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Politics_of_the_United_Kingdom "Politics of the United Kingdom"). [Anthony Eden](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Anthony_Eden "Anthony Eden") was accused of misleading parliament and resigned from office on 9 January 1957. Eden had been prime minister for less than two years when he resigned, and his unsuccessful handling of the Suez Crisis eclipsed the successes he had achieved in the previous 30 years as foreign secretary in three Conservative governments.[[268]](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Suez_Crisis#cite_note-275)

Eden's successor, Harold Macmillan, accelerated the process of decolonisation and sought to restore Britain's special relationship with the United States.[[269]](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Suez_Crisis#cite_note-276)[_[page needed](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Wikipedia:Citing\_sources "Wikipedia:Citing sources")_] He enjoyed a close friendship with Eisenhower, dating from the [North African campaign](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/North_African_Campaign#Operation_Torch "North African Campaign") in World War II, where General Eisenhower commanded allied invasion forces and Macmillan provided political liaison with [Winston Churchill](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Winston_Churchill "Winston Churchill").[[270]](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Suez_Crisis#cite_note-277) Benefiting from his personal popularity and a healthy economy, Macmillan's government increased its Parliamentary majority in the [1959 general election](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/1959_United_Kingdom_general_election "1959 United Kingdom general election").[[271]](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Suez_Crisis#cite_note-autogenerated449-278)

The Suez crisis, though a blow to British power in the Near East, did not mark its end. Britain intervened successfully in Jordan to put down riots that threatened the rule of King Hussein in 1958 and in 1961 deployed troops to Kuwait to successfully deter an Iraqi invasion. The latter deployment had been a response to the threats of the Iraqi dictator General [Abd al-Karim Qasim](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Abd_al-Karim_Qasim "Abd al-Karim Qasim") that he would invade and annex Kuwait. At the same time, though British influence continued in the Middle East, Suez was a blow to British prestige in the Near East from which the country never recovered.[[271]](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Suez_Crisis#cite_note-autogenerated449-278) Britain evacuated all positions [East of Suez](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/East_of_Suez "East of Suez") by 1971, though this was due mainly to economic factors.

Increasingly, British foreign policy thinking turned away from acting as a great imperial power. During the 1960s there was much speculation that Prime Minister [Harold Wilson](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Harold_Wilson "Harold Wilson")'s continued refusals to send British troops to the [Vietnam War](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Vietnam_War "Vietnam War"), even as a token force, despite President [Lyndon B. Johnson](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Lyndon_B._Johnson "Lyndon B. Johnson")'s persistent requests, were partially due to the Americans not supporting Britain during the Suez Crisis. [Edward Heath](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Edward_Heath "Edward Heath") was dismayed by the U.S. opposition to Britain during the Suez Crisis; as Prime Minister in October 1973 he refused the U.S. permission to use any of the UK's air bases to resupply during the [Yom Kippur War](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Yom_Kippur_War "Yom Kippur War"),[[272]](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Suez_Crisis#cite_note-279) or to allow the Americans to gather intelligence from [British bases in Cyprus](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Akrotiri_and_Dhekelia "Akrotiri and Dhekelia").[[273]](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Suez_Crisis#cite_note-280)

However, the British relationship with the United States did not suffer lasting consequences from the crisis. "The Anglo-American '[special relationship](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Special_Relationship "Special Relationship")' was revitalised immediately after the Suez Crisis", writes Risse Kappen.[[274]](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Suez_Crisis#cite_note-281) The United States wanted to restore the prestige of its closest ally and thus "The two governments...engaged in almost ritualistic reassurances that their 'special relationship' would be restored quickly". One example came with Britain's first [hydrogen bomb](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Thermonuclear_weapon "Thermonuclear weapon") test [Operation Grapple](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Operation_Grapple "Operation Grapple") which led to the [1958 U.S.–UK Mutual Defence Agreement](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/1958_U.S.%E2%80%93UK_Mutual_Defence_Agreement "1958 U.S.–UK Mutual Defence Agreement").[[275]](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Suez_Crisis#cite_note-282) Six years after the crisis, the Americans amazed the British by selling them state-of-the-art missile technology at a moderate cost, which became the [UK Polaris programme](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/UK_Polaris_programme "UK Polaris programme").[[276]](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Suez_Crisis#cite_note-jstor2009841-283)

The war led to the eviction of [GCHQ](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/GCHQ "GCHQ") from several of its best foreign [signals intelligence](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Signals_intelligence "Signals intelligence") collection sites, including [the new Perkar, Ceylon site](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Far_East_Combined_Bureau "Far East Combined Bureau"), recently developed at a cost of £2 million, equivalent to £66 million in 2023, and [RAF Habbaniya](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/RAF_Habbaniya "RAF Habbaniya"), Iraq.[[277]](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Suez_Crisis#cite_note-aldrich-2011-284)

### France

Risse-Kappen argued that [Franco-American ties](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/France%E2%80%93United_States_relations "France–United States relations") never recovered from the Suez crisis. There were various reasons for this. Previously there had already been strains in the Franco-American relationship triggered by what Paris considered U.S. betrayal of the French war effort in Indochina at [Dien Bien Phu](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Battle_of_Dien_Bien_Phu "Battle of Dien Bien Phu") in 1954.[[278]](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Suez_Crisis#cite_note-ReferenceA-285) According to Risse-Kappen, this incident demonstrated the weakness of the [NATO](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/NATO "NATO") alliance by not planning and co-operating beyond the European stage. Mollet believed Eden should have delayed calling the Cabinet together until 7 November, taking the whole canal in the meantime, and then veto with the French any UN resolution on sanctions.[[279]](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Suez_Crisis#cite_note-auto-286)

From the point of view of General [Charles de Gaulle](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Charles_de_Gaulle "Charles de Gaulle"), the Suez events demonstrated to France that it could not rely on its allies. The British had initiated a ceasefire in the midst of the battle without consulting the French, while the Americans had opposed Paris politically. The damage to the ties between Paris and Washington, D.C., "culminated in President de Gaulle's 1966 decision to withdraw from the military integration of NATO".[[279]](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Suez_Crisis#cite_note-auto-286)

The Suez war had an immense impact on French domestic politics. Much of the French Army officer corps felt that they been "betrayed" by what they considered to be the spineless politicians in Paris when they were on the verge of victory just as they believed they had been "betrayed" in Vietnam in 1954, and accordingly became more determined to win the war in Algeria, even if it meant overthrowing the [Fourth Republic](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/French_Fourth_Republic "French Fourth Republic") to do so. The Suez crisis thus helped to set the stage for the military disillusionment with the Fourth Republic, which was to lead to the [collapse of the republic](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/May_1958_crisis_in_France "May 1958 crisis in France") in 1958.[[280]](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Suez_Crisis#cite_note-Sowerwine,_Charles_p._278-287) According to the protocol of Sèvres agreements, France secretly transmitted parts of its [own atomic technology](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/France_and_weapons_of_mass_destruction "France and weapons of mass destruction") to Israel, including a detonator.[[281]](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Suez_Crisis#cite_note-288)

### Israel

[](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/File:Tiran_Guns_IMG_0937.JPG)

An Israeli soldier stands next to an Egyptian gun that had blocked the [Tiran Straits](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Tiran_Straits "Tiran Straits").

The Israel Defense Forces gained confidence from the campaign.[_[according to whom?](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Wikipedia:Manual\_of\_Style/Words\_to\_watch#Unsupported\_attributions "Wikipedia:Manual of Style/Words to watch")_] The war demonstrated that Israel was capable of executing large scale military manoeuvres in addition to small night-time raids and counter-insurgency operations. [David Ben-Gurion](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/David_Ben-Gurion "David Ben-Gurion"), reading on 16 November that 90,000 British and French troops had been involved in the Suez affair, wrote in his diary, 'If they had only appointed a commander of ours over this force, Nasser would have been destroyed in two days.'[[282]](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Suez_Crisis#cite_note-lrb.co.uk-289)

The war also had tangible benefits for Israel. The [Straits of Tiran](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Straits_of_Tiran "Straits of Tiran"), closed by Egypt since 1950,[[14]](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Suez_Crisis#cite_note-Pierre2014-19) were re-opened. Israeli shipping could henceforth move freely through the Straits of Tiran to and from Africa and Asia. The Israelis also secured the presence of UN Peacekeepers in Sinai. Operation Kadesh bought Israel an eleven-year lull on its southern border with Egypt.[[283]](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Suez_Crisis#cite_note-290)

Israel escaped the political humiliation that befell Britain and France following their swift, forced withdrawal. In addition, its stubborn refusal to withdraw without guarantees, even in defiance of the United States and United Nations, ended all Western efforts, mainly American and British ones, to impose a political settlement in the Middle East without taking Israel's security needs into consideration.[[284]](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Suez_Crisis#cite_note-Alteras-291)

In October 1965 Eisenhower told Jewish fundraiser and Republican party supporter [Max M. Fisher](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Max_M._Fisher "Max M. Fisher") that he greatly regretted forcing Israel to withdraw from the Sinai peninsula; Vice President Nixon recalled that Eisenhower expressed the same view to him on several occasions.[[284]](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Suez_Crisis#cite_note-Alteras-291)

### Canada

[Lester B. Pearson](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Lester_B._Pearson "Lester B. Pearson"), who would later become the [Prime Minister of Canada](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Prime_Minister_of_Canada "Prime Minister of Canada"), was awarded the [Nobel Peace Prize](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Nobel_Peace_Prize "Nobel Peace Prize") in 1957 for his efforts in creating a mandate for a United Nations Peacekeeping Force, and he is considered the father of the modern concept of [peacekeeping](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Peacekeeping "Peacekeeping").[[285]](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Suez_Crisis#cite_note-292) The Suez Crisis contributed to the adoption of a new national [flag of Canada](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Flag_of_Canada "Flag of Canada") in 1965, as the Egyptian government had objected to [Canadian peacekeeping](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Canadian_peacekeeping "Canadian peacekeeping") troops on the grounds that their flag at that time included a [British ensign](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/British_ensign "British ensign").[[286]](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Suez_Crisis#cite_note-293)[_[page needed](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Wikipedia:Citing\_sources "Wikipedia:Citing sources")_]

### Soviet Union

The Soviet Union, once outside what was considered a Western sphere of influence, was now a friend of the Arabs. Shortly after it reopened, the canal was traversed by the first [Soviet Navy](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Soviet_Navy "Soviet Navy") warships since [World War I](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/World_War_I "World War I"). The Soviets' burgeoning influence in the Middle East, although it was not to last, included acquiring Mediterranean bases, introducing multipurpose projects, supporting the budding [Palestinian liberation movement](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Palestinian_nationalism "Palestinian nationalism") and penetrating the Arab countries.[[287]](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Suez_Crisis#cite_note-militaria.forum-xl.com-294)[_[unreliable source?](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Wikipedia:Reliable\_sources "Wikipedia:Reliable sources")_]

5.   **[^](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Suez_Crisis#cite_ref-18 "Jump up")**From an [Intelligence](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Intelligence_assessment "Intelligence assessment") perspective and according to [CIA](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/CIA "CIA") original operative, [Miles Copeland](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Miles_Copeland_Jr. "Miles Copeland Jr."), CIA's internal and external disagreements (with its British counterparts) of the impending Suez Crisis rendered it to be one of indecision by the [western powers](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Western_world "Western world"); Copeland described the discoordination with the British Intelligence at the time as "Our British counterparts were apparently in ignorance of what my CIA team had been doing in Cairo during the previous two years". And the only political move of Nasser that the CIA did not acknowledge preemptively (against [Frank Wisner](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Frank_Wisner "Frank Wisner")'s insistences): "[Secretary Dulles](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/John_Foster_Dulles "John Foster Dulles") failed to understand rule number one: 'you can hardly win a game if you don't even know you're in one.' But a winning strategy can come to a sad end if it fails to take into account radical changes in the gameboard itself. Nasser used to say, 'I don't act; I only react.' That made it easy for us—what the hell, let's not mince words: made it easy for me. (one can be too self-effacing, you know.) Oh, yes, there was one move of Nasser's which [Kim](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Kermit_Roosevelt_Jr. "Kermit Roosevelt Jr.") and I both failed to predict. When Secretary Dulles announced that we weren't going to help Nasser with his Aswan Dam, we were called to a meeting at the State Department to help figure out how he would react. There were many suggestions, but only Frank Wisner, our beloved boss, mentioned the possibility of Nasser's nationalizing the Suez Canal Company. Kim and I both kicked him under the table (we loved Frank, and didn't want him to make a fool of himself), but he persisted as one or another of the State Department people sitting around the table explained to him, patronizingly, why such an action was unlikely. Well, as everybody now knows, Nasser did eventually nationalize the canal company (not the canal itself, as has been erroneously reported, but the company), and Frank called us to his office to crow. 'When you come', he said, 'would you please bring your notes on the State Department meeting.' Frank was in high I-told-you-so spirits—until he looked through the notes seeking a reference to his prediction. He couldn't find it! 'Don't you remember?' he said, his voice rising. 'I said two or three times how I thought Nasser might nationalize the canal company.' Kim looked at me; I looked at Kim. 'Frank, I don't remember you saying anything like that. Do you, Miles?' 'I didn't hear him,' I said to Kim, then to Frank, 'Are you sure you didn't just think about suggesting it? After all, it would have been a very prescient suggestion, but ...' 'You know I said it!' Frank kept insisting, but Kim and I, with bewildered looks on our faces, kept saying that we didn't remember. It was a dirty trick, and we've had guilty thoughts about it often, especially after Frank died of his own hand less than a year later after seeing his pet operation, the revolution in Hungary, go sour. I would like to go on record as saying that Frank Wisner unknown to most Americans, was a truly great man and a perfect boss. Stewart Alsop said that he 'died as much a victim of war as any soldier killed in battle', and his friends and underlings were 100 per cent in agreement."

"When nationalization of the Canal Company was announced, the British immediately took and held the initiatives. We played along with them despite our awareness that British intelligence, for all its superior competence throughout the rest of the Middle East, was grossly uninformed on all that had been going on inside the Nasser government and on the general situation in Egypt. In one of the what-to-do-about-Nasser meetings some of my CIA colleagues and I had with SIS officers a month or so before the Anglo—French—Israeli attack on Egypt, an officer showed me a highly secret document purporting to be a chart showing the organization of the Mukhabarat, the Egyptian intelligence service. I thought he was pulling my leg! It was the chart my [BA&H](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Booz_Allen_Hamilton "Booz Allen Hamilton") colleagues and I had drawn up, translated from the Arabic into what we Americans liked to call 'Anglicized English'. The interesting part was the list of the section heads, all friends of mine, some of them misspelled, some without first names, and some entirely wrong due to faulty interpretation of footnotes. Our British counterparts were apparently in ignorance of what my CIA team had been doing in Cairo during the previous two years. What bothered us most, however, was the fact that the British weren't reacting at all like seasoned, cold-blooded gameplayers. Everything our colleagues in SIS and the Foreign Office said to us showed that they had no information that made any sense at all on which Egyptian officers or civilians might constitute a new government if Nasser were to be eliminated, or on the general situation inside Egypt. They were only guessing and making assumptions. And they didn't seem to care. They thought they should just get rid of Nasser, hang the practical consequences, just to show the world that an upstart like him couldn't get away with so ostentatiously twisting the lion's tail. It was as though a chess Grand Master, embarrassed at having been outmaneuvered by an opponent whom he considered an inferior player, wanted to kick over the table."[[13]](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Suez_Crisis#cite_note-17)
6.   **[^](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Suez_Crisis#cite_ref-194 "Jump up")**"As late as 1956 it was the middle class, not the working class, who opposed the Anglo-French invasion of Egypt".[[188]](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Suez_Crisis#cite_note-193)
7.   **[^](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Suez_Crisis#cite_ref-261 "Jump up")**[CIA](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/CIA "CIA") original operative, [Miles Copeland](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Miles_Copeland_Jr "Miles Copeland Jr") later wrote: "When the dust had settled after the Suez affair, it was clear that we had made at least temporary gains on the international gameboard. Nasser emerged from it stronger and more popular than ever before, not only in Egypt but throughout the Middle East...I find it difficult to believe, but I have been told by sources in whom I have confidence that, at the UN, delegates from Third World countries were actually smiling at our delegates as they passed them in the halls. But it didn't last, because our way of capitalizing on [Ray Hare](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Raymond_A._Hare "Raymond A. Hare")'s suggestion that 'we must seize this opportunity to establish a strong position' was something called the 'Eisenhower Doctrine'. Ah, the Eisenhower Doctrine! Announced with the remarkable sense of timing we had come to associate with our Secretary of State, it was an offer by the US Government to commit American troops to the defence of any Middle Eastern government 'endangered by overt armed aggression from any nation controlled by international Communism'. At the time, there were no Middle Eastern nations controlled by international Communism, and no nations threatened by Communist aggression. On the contrary, the Soviets were offering arms, economic aid and political support to any Middle Eastern country that would accept. The Eisenhower Doctrine infuriated those Arab states which our political action campaigns were trying to bring into line, and only stimulated the prevailing inclinations to venality among our political mercenaries."[[254]](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Suez_Crisis#cite_note-FOOTNOTECopeland1989204%E2%80%93205-260)

67.   **[^](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Suez_Crisis#cite_ref-72 "Jump up")**[Neff 1981](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Suez_Crisis#CITEREFNeff1981), p.73
68.   **[^](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Suez_Crisis#cite_ref-Laron-p16_73-0 "Jump up")**Laron, Guy (February 2007). ["Cutting the Gordian Knot: The Post-WWII Egyptian Quest for Arms and the 1955 Czechoslovak Arms Deal"](http://www.wilsoncenter.org/publication/cutting-the-gordian-knot-the-post-wwii-egyptian-quest-for-arms-and-the-1955-czechoslovak). wilsoncenter.org. p.16. Egyptian representatives were able to sign a new commercial agreement with Czechoslovakia on 24 October 1951, which included a secret clause stating that "the government of Czechoslovakia will provide the Egyptian government with arms and ammunition—to be selected by Egyptian experts—worth about 600 million Egyptian pounds, to be paid in Egyptian cotton." The Egyptian experts requested 200 tanks, 200 armored vehicles, 60 to 100 MIG-15 planes, 2,000 trucks, 1,000 jeeps, and other items.... Czechoslovakia would not be able to supply weapons to Egypt in 1952. And each year, from then until 1955, Prague kept finding new reasons to delay the shipments

(1 December 1996). [_Arab and Israeli Terrorism: The Causes and Effects of Political Violence, 1936–1993_](https://books.google.com/books?id=QRXURzwdXS4C&pg=PA39). McFarland. pp.39–40. [ISBN](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/ISBN_(identifier) "ISBN (identifier)")[978-0-7864-3105-2](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Special:BookSources/978-0-7864-3105-2 "Special:BookSources/978-0-7864-3105-2"). Nasser was personally furious; the raid, using sophisticated weapons, had no provocation. Seeing that peace was impossible ... he also allowed Palestinians, who held sizeable demonstrations in Gaza and Cairo after the attack, to organize raids. ... These incursions paved the way for the 1956 Suez War...

108.   **[^](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Suez_Crisis#cite_ref-El-Hasan2010p156_113-0 "Jump up")**El-Hasan, Hasan Afif (1 January 2010). _Israel Or Palestine? Is the Two-state Solution Already Dead?: A Political and Military History of the Palestinian-Israeli Conflict_. Algora Publishing. p.[156](https://books.google.com/books?id=qywm7XKcRvgC&pg=PA156). [ISBN](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/ISBN_(identifier) "ISBN (identifier)")[978-0-8758-6794-6](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Special:BookSources/978-0-8758-6794-6 "Special:BookSources/978-0-8758-6794-6"). in 1955...The U.S offered to finance the High Dam on the Nile river...in exchange for Egypt's help to settle the Arab-Israeli conflict peacefully...But Nasser rejected the offer because it would mean siding with the West in the Cold War.... was the quasi alliance with the soviets more important than solving the Palestinian issue peacefully?...since the alternative to a negotiated settlement was a war with unpredictable consequences, Nasser's refusal to accept the Negotiation offer was irrational.

114.   **[^](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Suez_Crisis#cite_ref-PodehWinckler2004p105_119-0 "Jump up")**Podeh, Elie; Winckler, Onn (1 December 2004). [_Rethinking Nasserism: Revolution and Historical Memory in Modern Egypt_](https://books.google.com/books?id=1Z-po72YcH4C). University Press of Florida. pp.105, 106. [ISBN](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/ISBN_(identifier) "ISBN (identifier)")[978-0-8130-3137-8](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Special:BookSources/978-0-8130-3137-8 "Special:BookSources/978-0-8130-3137-8"). the prominent historian and commentator Abd al-Azim Ramadan, In a series of articles published in AlWafd, subsequently compiled in a book published in 2000, Ramadan criticized the Nasser cult.... The events leading up to the nationalization of the Suez Canal Company, as other events during Nasser's rule, Ramadan wrote, showed Nasser to be far from a rational, responsible leader.... His decision to nationalize the Suez Canal was his alone, made without political or military consultation. ... The source of all this evil. Ramadan noted, was Nasser's inclination to solitary decision making ... the revolutionary regime led by the same individual—Nasser— repeated its mistakes when it decided to expel the international peacekeeping force from the Sinai Peninsula and close the Straits of Tiran in 1967. Both decisions led to a state of war with Israel, despite the lack of military preparedness

152.   **[^](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Suez_Crisis#cite_ref-157 "Jump up")**Doyle, Col E. D. Retrieved 19 May 2022. In all 3,000 Egyptians were killed, 1,100 in Port Said about 800 of them civilians. The British lost 22 killed, and the Israelis 200. British accounts rarely mention civilian casualties.
